6:["$","main",null,{"className":"mx-auto mt-20 flex max-w-prose flex-col gap-4","children":[["$","header",null,{"className":"flex flex-col gap-8","children":[["$","a",null,{"className":"font-bold underline","href":"/blog","children":"Back to all writing"}],["$","p",null,{"children":"Feb. 6, 2025"}],["$","h2",null,{"className":"font-headings text-center text-2xl leading-[1] font-extralight uppercase md:text-4xl","children":"Why I rebuilt ProseMirror’s renderer in React"}],["$","p",null,{"className":"text-base font-bold md:text-xl","children":["by ","Shane Friedman"]}],["$","p",null,{"children":["Originally published to"," ",["$","a",null,{"className":"font-bold underline","href":"https://smoores.dev/post/why_i_rebuilt_prosemirror_view","children":"smoores.dev"}]]}]]}],[["$","section",null,{"id":"intro","className":"flex flex-col gap-4 text-base md:text-xl","children":[["$","p",null,{"children":[["$","span",null,{"className":"decoration-green text-lg font-bold underline underline-offset-8 md:text-2xl","children":"For almost five years"}],", I worked on the New York Times’ rich text editor, Oak. It’s used by over a thousand journalists at the Times to author nearly every story that’s published to the site. It needed to support Google Docs-style collaborative editing and a “what you see is what you get” (WYSIWYG) experience, so that journalists could work together and see how their writing was actually going to appear to readers."]}],["$","p",null,{"children":"As you might imagine, this was not a simple application. Setting aside the complexity incurred from building on top of a content management system that was over a decade old, Oak was software built for professionals, and it showed. It supported complex workflows and had a practically uncountable number of features. And, honestly, it was still very usable, and it even looked nice!"}],["$","p",null,{"children":["The Times’ website is written with React, which is one of the big reasons that Oak was written in React. We were able to author React components that could be used by both the reader-facing website"," ",["$","em",null,{"children":"and"}]," Oak, which meant that journalists were working in an editor that looked quite a lot like the reader-facing website."]}],["$","p",null,{"children":"But even without that advantage, React would have been a good choice for Oak. It was an app with a large amount of state, and that state drove a very complex user interface. In many ways, it was exactly the type of application that React was designed for."}],["$","p",null,{"children":["The other library that Oak was written with was ProseMirror. Nowadays, there are really wonderful libraries like Lexical and Slate.js for building rich text editors with React, but back in 2017, ProseMirror was basically the only game in town. And besides, ProseMirror is a brilliant library. ProseMirror documents are modeled around the notion of a Schema — a kind of blueprint for a document — that allows developers to declaratively define what kinds of content their editor can edit, and how that content can fit together. If you’re not familiar with ProseMirror, it may be worth taking a moment to read through"," ",["$","a",null,{"className":"font-bold underline","href":"https://prosemirror.net/docs/guide/","children":"the Library Guide"}]," ","to get a better sense of how it works."]}]]}],["$","section",null,{"id":"the-problem","className":"flex flex-col gap-4 text-base md:text-xl","children":[["$","p",null,{"children":[["$","span",null,{"className":"decoration-green text-lg font-bold underline underline-offset-8 md:text-2xl","children":"Unfortunately for us"}],", React and ProseMirror do not play nicely together. When I first joined the Oak team, in 2019, my onboarding included a several-hour whiteboarding session on Oak’s frontend architecture. This included a deep dive into “The Seam,” the adapter layer that we’d built between React and ProseMirror. The Seam was… gnarly. Everyone had their suspicions that it wasn’t"," ",["$","em",null,{"children":"quite"}]," correct, in the formal sense, and that the transient, hard-to-reproduce editing bugs that users often reported were probably due to something in its implementation. I remember this whiteboarding session clearly, because it was in that session, on my second day at the Times, that I decided what I was going to do. I was going to fix The Seam."]}],["$","p",null,{"children":["It turned out, this was very much not a one-engineer kind of job. Instead, over the next four years, our whole team learned the ins and outs of both React and ProseMirror. We deepened our knowledge, even going as far as to produce"," ",["$","a",null,{"className":"font-bold underline","href":"https://nytimes.github.io/oak-byo-react-prosemirror-redux/","children":"educational materials for new team members"}],". And over time, we became able to pinpoint precisely where React and ProseMirror disagreed, and started to come up with plans for better solutions."]}],["$","p",null,{"children":"As it happens, React and ProseMirror actually have very similar philosophies about how to manage the DOM. Both libraries maintain a sort of “virtual DOM”, a tree structure that’s very efficient to update, which maps to the actual DOM that the browser uses to render the web page. Both libraries also separate state from the view, and use that state to drive the view unidirectionally. The differences between how they implement these philosophies are small, but they’re very important."}],["$","p",null,{"children":"React separates updates into two phases. Phase one is the “render” phase, and phase two is the “commit” phase. In the render phase, React traverses the component tree to update the virtual DOM. Because updating the virtual DOM is very fast (at least, relative to updating the actual DOM), the entire virtual DOM is computed from scratch (with some exceptions) during each render phase. Then, during the commit phase, React traverses the virtual DOM, finds places where it differs from the actual DOM, and uses this diff to make minimal updates the actual DOM."}],["$","p",null,{"children":"This flow is strictly unidirectional — React expects all changes to happen to state first, and then it will translate those state updates into view updates. If React-managed DOM is directly modified outside the React lifecycle, React will revert those changes during the commit phase."}],["$","p",null,{"children":["ProseMirror renders and commits all at once. Because it’s common to dispatch a transaction (a description of a change) and then immediately inspect the document to, e.g., update the placement of a widget, all updates to the ProseMirror synchronously update the DOM. Also, the relationship between ProseMirror state and the view is not"," ",["$","em",null,{"children":"strictly"}]," unidirectional — because of how complex and underspecified text editing behavior is in web browsers, ProseMirror often allows the browser to handle a change, then inspects the document to determine what change happened."]}]]}],["$","section",null,{"id":"first-attempt","className":"flex flex-col gap-4 text-base md:text-xl","children":[["$","h3",null,{"className":"font-headings text-xl leading-[1] font-extralight uppercase md:text-3xl","children":"v1"}],["$","p",null,{"children":[["$","span",null,{"className":"decoration-green text-lg font-bold underline underline-offset-8 md:text-2xl","children":"Let’s walk through"}]," a first approach at integrating React and ProseMirror, so that we can feel out the issues at play. The first thing that we want to be able to do is render the ProseMirror EditorView, the component responsible for managing the actual interactive text editor, from within a React component. We’ll need to construct the EditorView in an effect hook for two reasons:"]}],["$","ol",null,{"children":[["$","li",null,{"children":["$","p",null,{"children":["The EditorView needs to be provided with a DOM node to mount the editor on, just like ReactDOM’s"," ","$L14"," function. That means we need to hold off on creating it until after React has committed changes to the DOM."]}]}],["$","li",null,{"children":["$","p",null,{"children":"Creating and updating the EditorView will produce “side effects.” Specifically, the EditorView will modify the real DOM."}]}]],"className":"ml-4 list-decimal"}],"$L15",[["$","p",null,{"children":"Try it out!"}],["$","div",null,{"className":"group/demo rounded-sm border bg-gray-200 p-4 focus-within:border-black","children":["$","$L16",null,{"children":["$","$L17",null,{}]}]}]],["$","p",null,{"children":"This actually works fairly well! And indeed, many React/ProseMirror integrations look just like this. It works just fine for simple editors, but things start to get more complex as we add features. Let’s try adding a simple tooltip that shows the user what position they’re at in the document."}],["$","p",null,{"className":"text-sm","children":"Note: if we were building this feature without React, we would use a ProseMirror plugin that implemented a widget decoration or a plugin view for this tooltip. But, as mentioned earlier, in any other context, this is exactly the kind of feature it makes sense to reach for React to build, and the premise of this integration is that we should be able to use React to add more complex features to our editor."}],"$L18",[["$","p",null,{"children":"Try it out!"}],["$","div",null,{"className":"group/demo rounded-sm border bg-gray-200 p-4 focus-within:border-black","children":["$","$L16",null,{"children":["$","$L19",null,{}]}]}]],["$","p",null,{"children":["… Huh. That didn’t work at all! At the moment, the entire ProseMirror integration lives in a ref (","$L1a","), so it doesn’t trigger a render when it updates. To fix this, we need to"," ",["$","em",null,{"children":"lift"}]," the EditorState out of the EditorView and into React state, so that it will trigger a re-render when it’s updated. Let’s give that a shot. We’ll create a new React state hook to hold the EditorState, and use ProseMirror’s `view.updateState()` API to update the editor after the EditorState changes."]}],"$L1b",[["$","p",null,{"children":"Try it out!"}],["$","div",null,{"className":"group/demo rounded-sm border bg-gray-200 p-4 focus-within:border-black","children":["$","$L16",null,{"children":["$","$L1c",null,{}]}]}]],["$","p",null,{"children":"This is much better! The tooltip actually renders, for one thing. And as you move your cursor around, the tooltip follows, just like we intended. There’s a pretty big issue, though. Try typing something at the end of the last sentence. The cursor positioning totally breaks!"}],["$","p",null,{"children":["We’re again running into React’s two-phase update cycle. We call"," ","$L1d"," during the"," ",["$","em",null,{"children":"render"}]," phase, but that method inspects the DOM to determine its coordinates. Because we’re in the render phase, the DOM hasn’t been updated yet! Just like when we set up the EditorView, we need to move this call into an effect hook so that it executes at the end of the commit phase, after the DOM has been updated."]}],"$L1e",[["$","p",null,{"children":"Try it out!"}],["$","div",null,{"className":"group/demo rounded-sm border bg-gray-200 p-4 focus-within:border-black","children":["$","$L16",null,{"children":["$","$L1f",null,{}]}]}]],["$","p",null,{"children":["Still no dice. The positioning still breaks when the document changes! The issue here is that the DOM that"," ","$L20"," is inspecting isn’t owned by React at all — it’s owned by ProseMirror! We update ProseMirror’s state and DOM in a layout effect in the"," ","$L21"," component, which is executed ",["$","em",null,{"children":"after"}]," the layout effect in the"," ","$L22"," component."]}],["$","p",null,{"children":"Fixing this is going to take some thinking, so we’ll just take note of it for now and come back to it later. We may as well start a list, as we’re going to run into a few of these."}],["$","h3",null,{"className":"font-headings text-lg leading-[1] font-extralight uppercase md:text-2xl","children":"To-do list"}],["$","div",null,{"className":"flex gap-2","children":[["$","input",null,{"className":"mt-[1.375rem] self-start","disabled":true,"type":"checkbox","checked":"$undefined"}],["$","p",null,{"children":["Devise a system for deferring layout effects in child components until"," ",["$","em",null,{"children":"after"}]," the EditorView has updated its DOM"]}]]}]]}],["$","section",null,{"id":"state-tearing","className":"flex flex-col gap-4 text-base md:text-xl","children":[["$","p",null,{"children":[["$","span",null,{"className":"decoration-green text-lg font-bold underline underline-offset-8 md:text-2xl","children":"Before we move on"}],", there’s one more subtle issue here that’s worth exploring. When moved the EditorState into React state, we updated our ","$L23"," ","component to read the current selection from this new state value, instead of from the EditorView. What would have happened if we’d left it as is, reading from the EditorView’s state?"]}],"$L24",[["$","p",null,{"children":"Try it out!"}],["$","div",null,{"className":"group/demo rounded-sm border bg-gray-200 p-4 focus-within:border-black","children":["$","$L16",null,{"children":["$","$L25",null,{}]}]}]],["$","p",null,{"children":["Funky, right? The tooltip only moves after every ",["$","em",null,{"children":"other"}]," ","selection change. The underlying issue here is one of"," ",["$","strong",null,{"children":"state tearing"}],". During the render phase of this component, depending on where you look, you can find two different versions of the EditorState. If you look in React state, you’ll find the newest version — the state that’s about to be committed to the DOM. But if you look in ProseMirror state, you’ll find the previous version — the state that matches most recent DOM commit."]}],["$","p",null,{"children":"This is going to be another challenge to address, and one that will show up in slightly different forms throughout this journey. Let’s add it to our to-do list:"}],["$","h3",null,{"className":"font-headings text-lg leading-[1] font-extralight uppercase md:text-2xl","children":"To-do list"}],["$","div",null,{"className":"flex gap-2","children":[["$","input",null,{"className":"mt-[1.375rem] self-start","disabled":true,"type":"checkbox","checked":"$undefined"}],["$","p",null,{"children":["Devise a system for deferring layout effects in child components until"," ",["$","em",null,{"children":"after"}]," the EditorView has updated its DOM"]}]]}],["$","div",null,{"className":"flex gap-2","children":[["$","input",null,{"className":"mt-[1.375rem] self-start","disabled":true,"type":"checkbox","checked":"$undefined"}],["$","p",null,{"children":"Ensure that ProseMirror’s state can only be accessed when it matches React’s state"}]]}]]}],["$","section",null,{"id":"node-views","className":"flex flex-col gap-4 text-base md:text-xl","children":[["$","p",null,{"children":[["$","span",null,{"className":"decoration-green text-lg font-bold underline underline-offset-8 md:text-2xl","children":"Assuming we can address these issues"}],", we have a rough idea of how to render a ProseMirror editor within React. What about rendering a React component within a ProseMirror editor? The ability to develop custom, stateful node views is one of ProseMirror’s biggest draws for the Oak team. It allowed us to build an editor where the controls were always local to the part of the document that they controlled, which in turn made the editor much more intuitive to use for our journalists."]}],["$","p",null,{"children":"As an example, let’s build a simple custom node view for an image node that allows us to choose whether an image is left-aligned, right-aligned, or centered. We want the interface for controlling the image alignment to live right below the actual image it applies to, so that it’s clear to the user what will happen when they take an action."}],"$L26",[["$","p",null,{"children":"Try it out!"}],["$","div",null,{"className":"group/demo rounded-sm border bg-gray-200 p-4 focus-within:border-black","children":["$","$L16",null,{"children":["$","$L27",null,{}]}]}]],["$","p",null,{"children":["Let’s break that down a bit. First of all, we’re creating a custom ProseMirror node view. That looks like a function that takes the node and EditorView as arguments, along with a few others, and returns an object that implements the ","$L28"," API. Importantly, this NodeView object must have a"," ","$L29"," property that is synchronously set to a valid DOM node. This introduces a challenge right off the bat: ReactDOM’s ","$L2a"," function isn’t synchronous!"]}],["$","p",null,{"children":["Essentially all React/ProseMirror integrations work around this constraint the way we do here: by producing a wrapping"," ","$L2b"," element to hand to ProseMirror, and then asynchronously mounting the React component into that element. This turns out to be surprisingly fraught, as default browser editing behavior"," ",["$","a",null,{"className":"font-bold underline","href":"https://github.com/nytimes/react-prosemirror/issues/42","children":"tends to delete these wrapping elements in some cases"}],", but we’re going to stick with it for now!"]}],["$","p",null,{"children":"We should also take a look at how we’re actually rendering our React component. This approach creates a new React tree, rooted at our wrapper div, for each node. So if there are thirty images in our document, we have thirty React trees. These trees are disjointed — their updates aren’t tied together in any way, and we can’t share context between them. This means that if we wanted to use a global state management library, like Redux or React Query, we would have to wrap each node view in its own top-level provider."}],["$","p",null,{"children":["It also means that DOM updates happen asynchronously, and we can’t predict when they’ll be committed.
